        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202305019')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="dbca62f46b204e42ae29f31dc78fa37c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1f6ff513-3ec3-48c6-b4c5-753e72178160'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d49166966" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na-based layered transition metal oxides with an O3-type structure are considered promising cathodes for sodium-ion batteries. However, rapid capacity fading, and poor rate performance caused by serious structural changes and interfacial degradation hamper their use. In this study, a NaPO<sub>3</sub> surface modified O3-type layered NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is synthesized, with improved high-voltage stability through protecting layer against acid attack, which is achieved by a solid-gas reaction between the cathode particles and gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. The NaPO<sub>3</sub> nanolayer on the surface effectively stabilizes the crystal structure by inhibiting surface parasitic reactions and increasing the observed average voltage. Superior cyclic stability is exhibited by the surface-modified cathode (80.1%&nbsp;vs 63.6%) after 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C in the wide voltage range of 2.0&nbsp;V–4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Moreover, benefiting from the inherent ionic conduction of NaPO<sub>3</sub>, the surface-modified cathode presents excellent rate capability (103&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;vs 60&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 10&nbsp;C. The outcome of this study demonstrates a practically relevant approach to develop high rate and durable sodium-ion battery technology.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305019-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305019-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Electrochemical storage systems, such as batteries and super capacitors, have shown wide applications in new energy vehicles and renewable energy plants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0001_R_d49166958e475" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, lithium-ion batteries play key roles in distributed energy storage, power grid peak shaving, and frequency modulation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0002_R_d49166958e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0003_R_d49166958e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Energy Chem.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, with increasing demand for lithium, the current situation of lithium resource shortage will become more severe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0004_R_d49166958e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Power Sources">4</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0007_R_d49166958e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew Chem Int Ed Engl">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with lithium sources, sodium reserves are much more earth-abundant and geographically widespread. Meanwhile, sodium has analogous physical and chemical properties with lithium, which makes sodium-ion batteries a reasonable low-cost sustainable alternative to current lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0008_R_d49166958e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">8</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0012_R_d49166958e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The theoretical capacities of the cathodes are always less than those of the anodes at present, which greatly confines the key performance index of sodium-ion batteries such as energy density, cycling performance, and cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0013_R_d49166958e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Carbon Energy">13</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0015_R_d49166958e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is critical to explore high-performance cathode materials to boom the development of sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0016_R_d49166958e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small Methods">16</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0018_R_d49166958e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>O3-type layered transition metal oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, 0.8 &lt; x ≤ 1; TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, or mixtures of them) are considered one of the most appealing candidates because of their high theoretical capacity, tunable composition, and facile synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0018_R_d49166958e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0021_R_d49166958e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Carbon Energy">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various reported Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials, cobalt-free O3-type layered NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is one of the most promising positive cathode candidates with an appreciable capacity of 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (0.1&nbsp;C) and facile to synthesize, since first reported by Johnson and co-workers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0022_R_d49166958e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further investigations demonstrated that O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> could be used as cathode material to fabricate 1-Ah pouch cell sodium-ion batteries, which showed good cycling stability, with capacity retention over 73% after 500 cycles at 1&nbsp;C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0023_R_d49166958e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0024_R_d49166958e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chuneng Kexue Yu Jishu">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large-scale practical application of the layered cathodes at present is mainly limited by a few inherent drawbacks like insufficient electrochemical performance, high sensitivity to humidity, and prone to irreversible phase transitions at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0025_R_d49166958e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0028_R_d49166958e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Sci. China Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To tackle the above challenges from the O3-type layered cathodes, numerous strategies have been employed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0029_R_d49166958e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Rev.">29</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0033_R_d49166958e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Surface modification has proven an effective strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance of the O3-type cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0034_R_d49166958e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0035_R_d49166958e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Coating materials like ZrO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0036_R_d49166958e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chin. J. Proc. Eng.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> B<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0037_R_d49166958e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chuneng Kexue Yu Jishu">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and AlF<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0038_R_d49166958e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been used to prevent the dissolution of transition metal ions and alleviate the unfavorable side reactions taking place on the cathode interfaces significantly, thus improving the cell performance in terms of moisture stability and cycle stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0029_R_d49166958e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Rev.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0030_R_d49166958e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">30</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0039_R_d49166958e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, most coating materials exhibit poor ionic conductivity, resulting in an increase in surface/interface charge transfer resistance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0040_R_d49166958e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, Li and co-workers utilized Na<sub>3</sub>Zr<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>12</sub> (NZSP) to modify the particle surface via a sol-gel process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0041_R_d49166958e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Energy Chem.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The NZSP coating layer with excellent ion conductivity effectively prevents direct contact between cathode and electrolyte, guarantees stable phase interfaces, and accelerates Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion at the interface.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, sodium metaphosphate (NaPO<sub>3</sub>) bearing a good ionic/electronic conductivity is applied to coat the surface of the O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode, whose ionic conductivity is sufficiently high (≈10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and the chemical stability is firm to protect the oxide layer from acid attack at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0042_R_d49166958e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">42</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0044_R_d49166958e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Chem. Eng. J.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To develop a uniform nanoscale NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating, a facile solid-gas reaction process is applied, where gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> reacts with NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and sodium residual compounds (SRCs, like NaOH and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, etc.) on the particle surface (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0001">1</a>). Different from the conventional methods, this solid–gas reaction produces a continuous uniform coating layer as well as eliminates the electrochemically detrimental SRCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0045_R_d49166958e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eng. J.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the structural stability and electrochemical performance of the NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode are significantly improved within the voltage range of 2.0&nbsp;V–4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Moreover, the modified cathode demonstrated excellent cycling stability as well as rate capability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a84c2753-c14c-4b09-a56a-e4dbd0cae639/smll202305019-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a84c2753-c14c-4b09-a56a-e4dbd0cae639/smll202305019-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/62f0d0d8-e804-47b1-9ea7-c55bb4e8244c/smll202305019-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a84c2753-c14c-4b09-a56a-e4dbd0cae639/smll202305019-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic surface coating process of the O3-type cathode via the solid-gas reaction and working mechanism of surface coating layer for improved electrochemical performance.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305019-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305019-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202305019-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure and Morphology</h3>
            
            <p>The SEM images of the bare NFM, 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated, and 1NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM particles are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0002">2</a>. These three samples show similar spherical morphology, which is formed by the accumulation of plate-like primary particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0002">2a–c</a>), with a diameter of 14&nbsp;µm–15&nbsp;µm. The surface treatment does not affect the overall spherical morphology of the particles. With higher magnifications, scaly debris impurities are observed on the surface of the primary particles in bare NFM as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0002">2d</a>. These impurities are ascribed to sodium residual compounds (SRCs, like NaOH and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, etc.). The formation of SRCs is mainly attributed to the chemical reaction between H<sub>2</sub>O/CO<sub>2</sub> in the air and excessive Na<sub>2</sub>O remaining from sodium insertion process, which can be dislodged by water washing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0046_R_d49166958e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Lett.">46</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0048_R_d49166958e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nat. Commun.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A Smooth and clean surface of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM particle is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0002">2e</a>, indicating that the SRCs have been successfully removed after reacting with a tiny amount of gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Besides that, the surface of the primary particles of 1NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM is clearly coated with a layer of glassy substances as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0002">2c</a>, which implies that the amount of the coating material is definitely too much. The SEM images confirm that the SRCs and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> react successfully through the solid–gas mechanism on NFM particles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/951a3cd6-510d-429f-9cad-c6a910f3e730/smll202305019-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/951a3cd6-510d-429f-9cad-c6a910f3e730/smll202305019-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3211cfc4-ff10-491b-9413-ecef830c5ee0/smll202305019-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/951a3cd6-510d-429f-9cad-c6a910f3e730/smll202305019-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of different samples at different magnifications: a,d) NFM, b,e) 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM, and c,f) 1 NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>HRTEM is employed to further investigate the surface morphology of different samples. A smooth surface can be observed with the bare NFM as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0003">3a</a>. However, 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM exhibits a glassy amorphous coating layer outside and a layered phase inside (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0003">3c</a>). The outside amorphous layer is ascribed to the sodium metaphosphate coating and the inside layered phase is assigned to the intrinsic structure of NFM. The HRTEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0003">3b,d</a>) exhibit well-preserved lamellar lattice stripes in the selected regions with a crystal plane spacing of 5.35&nbsp;Å, corresponding to the (003) plane of bulk NFM.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0049_R_d49166958e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The EDS mapping images of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM show that Ni, Fe, Mn, Na, and P elements are evenly distributed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0003">3e</a>). XRD and Rietveld refinement is carried out to examine the influence of surface reconstruction on the general crystal structure including crystallinity, lattice parameters, and phase purity. The XRD data of NFM and modified samples shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0003">3f,g</a> revealed that the cathode materials had a well-ordered O3-phase with a R-3m space group and lattice parameters of a = 2.975(2)&nbsp;Å and c = 16.006(2)&nbsp;Å (R<sub>WP</sub> = 2.94) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), without an impurity phase and peak shifting.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0032_R_d49166958e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thermal treatment at 500&nbsp;°C does not drastically alter the crystal structure and lattice parameters (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the surface modification doesn't change the main crystal structure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/effe523c-a2d2-4cf1-9d0f-2886214ff9a9/smll202305019-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/effe523c-a2d2-4cf1-9d0f-2886214ff9a9/smll202305019-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15b437aa-fc2e-4479-adf6-63ff84d90432/smll202305019-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/effe523c-a2d2-4cf1-9d0f-2886214ff9a9/smll202305019-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM images of a,b) NFM, c,d) 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM; e) EDS mapping of Ni, Fe, Mn, Na, and P observed from the marked area of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM powder; Rietveld refinement of the XRD data for f) bare NFM and g) 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To characterize the component of the outermost thin surface layers after the gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> reaction, high-resolution XPS analysis is further performed. The peak located at 1072.0&nbsp;eV for the Na 1s spectrum is assigned to the metal–oxygen bonds, demonstrating the existence of Na─O chemical bond (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0004">4a</a>). There are two peaks at 133.2 and 134.6&nbsp;eV corresponding to P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0004">4b</a>), indicating the presence of sodium polyphosphate. The relative atomic ratio of the element distribution in the cathode surface is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Besides, Raman spectroscopy is further applied to characterize the structures of the bare and NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM powders. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0004">4c</a>, both samples display three sharp peaks near 580, 490, and 385&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, which originate from the A<sub>1g</sub> and E<sub>1g</sub> modes of TM−O vibrations and the E<sub>2g</sub> mode of Na vibrations of the layered hexagonal R-3m symmetry, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0050_R_d49166958e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Angew Chem Int Ed Engl">50</a>-<a href="#smll202305019-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0052_R_d49166958e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The spectrum of the NFM has a bump at 1100–1200&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> that may be originated from a second-order Raman scattering of A<sub>1g</sub> band frequency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0053_R_d49166958e918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noticing the appearance of new peak for the NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM at 1160&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, which stems from (PO<sub>2</sub>) symmetric stretching vibrations associated with the terminal oxygen species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0054_R_d49166958e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Mater. Chem.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the above results suggest that the gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> reacts with the SCRs on the outermost surface at 500&nbsp;°C, forming a thin sodium metaphosphate coating layer on the surface of the NFM particle.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2c9d2d6a-a2b4-48d4-be05-ea4a078c4da6/smll202305019-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2c9d2d6a-a2b4-48d4-be05-ea4a078c4da6/smll202305019-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d83046fc-4008-4231-81cd-587666d0e9b5/smll202305019-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2c9d2d6a-a2b4-48d4-be05-ea4a078c4da6/smll202305019-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS analysis of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM powders: a) survey, b) P 2p, c) Raman spectroscopy results and d) XPS C 1 s spectra of the bare NFM and 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM powders.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The component change of the outermost thin surface layer after reaction with the gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> is further investigated by high-resolution XPS. According to the C 1s spectrum shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0004">4d</a>, the two peaks at 284.8 and 289.6&nbsp;eV are associated with reference carbon and carbonates, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0037_R_d49166958e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chuneng Kexue Yu Jishu">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbonates are readily produced on the surfaces of sodium transition metal oxides after exposure to air.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0055_R_d49166958e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Adv. Energy Mater.">55</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0056_R_d49166958e980" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These residual basic compounds promote side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte, increasing the polarization and the interfacial resistance accordingly. Moreover, the surface residual base destroys the electrode surface structure and ultimately deteriorates its electrochemical properties. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0004">4d</a>, the intensity of the peak at 289.6&nbsp;eV of the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathode material is apparently lower than that of the bare NFM material, indicating that sodium metaphosphate coating can reduce the content of carbonate on the surface of the particles.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202305019-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of the synthesized cathodes are evaluated using CR2032-type coin cells with a voltage range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and current density of 150&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (1&nbsp;C) at 28&nbsp;°C. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0005">5a</a> exhibits the first charge–discharge profiles at 0.1&nbsp;C for all three samples. The bare and NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated electrodes present similar charge and discharge behavior. Nevertheless, a notable difference is observed regarding the discharge capacity at the first buffer cycle, where more capacities are reduced by the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode (<i>ca</i>. 175&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 1NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode (<i>ca</i>. 167&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) compared to that of the bare electrode (≈190&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The decreasing discharge capacity of NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode is attributed to the augment of NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating layer and the diminished proportion of the active substances.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2968911-6e37-492f-966b-fe050ba50d42/smll202305019-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2968911-6e37-492f-966b-fe050ba50d42/smll202305019-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a118ff8-d2d0-4146-8bb1-3060db7ca32f/smll202305019-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2968911-6e37-492f-966b-fe050ba50d42/smll202305019-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of half-cell: a) initial charge/discharge curves, b) rate performance and c) cycling performance of bare NFM and modified NFM at 1&nbsp;C for 150 cycles in the voltage range of 2.0&nbsp;V–4.2&nbsp;V at 28&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Due to the inherently high Na-ion conductivity of NaPO<sub>3</sub>, the NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated cathodes are supposed to possess outstanding rate capabilities. It is noteworthy that the discharge-specific capacity at high C-rates is significantly increased for NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM compared with the bare NFM sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0005">5b</a>). In detail, the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode displays a discharge capacity of 103&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;C. In contrast, the rate performance of bare NFM is poor, which presents a discharge capacity of only 60&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;C. The enhanced rate capability suggests that the NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating layer promotes Na-ion diffusion due to the excellent Na-ion kinetics. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0005">5c</a> shows the cycling performance of the bare and surface-modified NFM. A formation process at a low rate is applied for the first four cycles to prevent overcharge. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0005">5c</a>, although the pristine NFM cathode shows the highest initial discharge capacity, a rapid decrease from 155 to 98&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is observed along with increasing cycles, leading to a capacity retention of only 63.6% after 150 cycles. On the contrary, the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathode delivers a high-capacity retention of 80.1% after 150 cycles.</p>
            
            <p>By comparing the morphology and electrochemical performance of the three samples, it can be concluded that 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM with an enhanced crystallinity and thin coating layer delivers better performance in terms of reversible capacity and cyclic stability. The enhanced crystallinity and thin coating layer promote sodium ion transportation, leading to better electrochemical kinetics. For this reason, the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode is focused for further studies.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) compares the difference in average discharge voltage between NFM and 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM with cycling. The increase of polarization during the cycle can be reflected by the attenuation of the average discharge voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0049_R_d49166958e1097" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The average discharge voltage of the bare NFM electrode drops obviously from 3.16 to 2.85&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with a retention ratio of 90% after 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. On the contrary, the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM electrode exhibits eminent high voltage retention of 94%, which indicates evidently lower polarization increase upon cycling. These results imply that appropriate NaPO<sub>3</sub> surface modification can significantly decrease polarization upon cycling.</p>
            
            <p>To further elucidate the polarization characteristics and phase change of different cathodes during long-term cycling, the dQ/dV profiles of NFM and 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathodes are obtained by differentiating the 1st/10th/50th/100th charge/discharge curves at 1&nbsp;C-rate shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0006">6</a>. The different initial charge/discharge curve plateaus observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0005">5a</a> correspond to three pairs of redox peaks in dQ/dV profiles. A pair of sharp peaks at 2.8/3.1&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na are observed, which mainly correlate to the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction. The other broad pair of peaks ≈3.5/3.7&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na is ascribed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> redox couple. When the cutoff voltage is set to 4.2&nbsp;V, both Ni and Fe are electrochemically active, and the redox couples of Ni<sup>3+</sup>/ Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> are mainly present in the charge voltage range from 4.0 to 4.3&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0031_R_d49166958e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Sci. China Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0057_R_d49166958e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Mater. Today Commun.">57</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0058_R_d49166958e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the capacity of the coated electrode in the high-voltage region above 4.0&nbsp;V is largely retained, whereas the bare electrode suffers from capacity fading, not only in the high-voltage region but also in the low-voltage region below 2.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0006">6a,b</a>). In addition, the voltage plateau observed at ≈2.5&nbsp;V completely disappears after the first 5 cycles, illustrating that a small fraction of O/P composite survives the initial charging/discharging cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0059_R_d49166958e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3270a346-3d17-472d-b621-717533e43332/smll202305019-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3270a346-3d17-472d-b621-717533e43332/smll202305019-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/861deddd-12f4-4eee-8012-5b5385439afc/smll202305019-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3270a346-3d17-472d-b621-717533e43332/smll202305019-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Charge/discharge profiles a,b) of different cycles at 1&nbsp;C-rate and corresponding dQ/dV profiles c,d) of bare NFM (a,c) and 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM (b,d).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>During charging, the O3-type layered oxide cathode materials undergo a series of phase changes: O3<sub>hex</sub> → P3<sub>hex</sub> → (P′3<sub>mon</sub>), which can be distributed to the three redox peaks in the dQ/dV profiles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0058_R_d49166958e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Energy Mater.">58</a>, <a href="#smll202305019-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0060_R_d49166958e1189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sharper peaks observed at ≈3.1&nbsp;V are attributed to the first phase transition from hexagonal O3 to hexagonal P3 phase. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0006">6c</a>, the first redox peak of NFM shifts rightward with increasing cycles, which indicates an amplified overpotential caused by electrode polarization. In comparison, the redox peaks of the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM sample shift as well as the peak intensity decays significantly. Significant differences between the two cathodes are observed at the end of the charge state. This demonstrates that the fast ion-conducting coating layer can effectively reduce the polarization and irreversibility of the NFM electrode upon cycling. The superior electrochemical performance of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM has manifested the effect of sodium metaphosphate coating in facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, restraining irreversible phase change, and retaining reversible capacity.</p>
            
            <p>XPS is used to investigate the components at the surface of the cathode before and after 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C to understand the details of the surface reaction triggered by NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating layers (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0007">7a</a>). Before cycling, the identical peak of 688&nbsp;eV (C-F) in the F 1s spectrum attributes to the binder PVDF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0061_R_d49166958e1215" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The binding energy peak appearing at 685.3&nbsp;eV is a signature of MF<sub>x</sub> (Metal fluorides, NaF, etc.), which is attributed to the reaction between active materials and fluorine-containing acidic species (like HF and PF<sub>5</sub>, which are generated in the decomposition of the electrolytic salt, NaPF<sub>6</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0043_R_d49166958e1227" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0007">7b</a>, the higher intensity of the NaF binding energy peak of the cycled NFM cathode indicates that the surface drastically degenerates during cycling, resulting in inferior electrochemical performance. The lower intensity of the NaF binding energy peak of 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathode confirms the significant success of the NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating in protecting the active species during the long cycling process. In addition, the morphology change of the cathode after 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C is further characterized by SEM. The NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathode particles shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) are less broken and powdery compared to the bare samples.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4ec3e58-febb-4ca6-bf0b-8ea828156229/smll202305019-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4ec3e58-febb-4ca6-bf0b-8ea828156229/smll202305019-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/48f047cb-46f2-4a34-b1e9-7d835baf1ca2/smll202305019-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4ec3e58-febb-4ca6-bf0b-8ea828156229/smll202305019-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of F 1s before (a,c) and after (b,d) 150 cycles at 1&nbsp;C: a,b) NFM and c,d) 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To examine the influence of NaPO<sub>3</sub> surface modification on the kinetics behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>, GITT (galvanostatic intermittent titration technique) is carried out to determine the apparent diffusion capability of Na<sup>+</sup> (D<sub>Na</sub>) in the initial charge/discharge process based on the equation introduced by Weppner and Huggins:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0062_R_d49166958e1278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Solid State Chem.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305019-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>M</mi>
                                             <mi>B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mi>S</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>S</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mi>t</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">≪</mo>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>L</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mi>D</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{D}_{N{a}^ + } = \frac{4}{{{\mathrm{\pi \tau }}}}{\left( {\frac{{{m}_{B\ }{V}_M}}{{{M}_BS}}} \right)}^2{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {E}_S}}{{\Delta {E}_t}}} \right)}^2\left( {\tau \ll {L}^2/D} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where D is the apparent diffusion coefficient (cm<sup>2</sup>s<sup>−1</sup>), τ represents the time of constant current, S represents the effective contact area between the cathode material and the electrolyte which is considered to be the area of the cathode disk, m<sub>B</sub>, V<sub>M</sub>, M<sub>B</sub>, and L represent the mass of active material, molecular volume, molar weight, and thickness of the electrode, respectively. ΔE<sub>S</sub> is a steady-state (balanced) voltage change and ΔE<sub>t</sub> illustrates the change of voltage when standing still.</div>
            
            <p>As NaPO<sub>3</sub> acts as a Na<sup>+</sup> conductor, the coating layer affects the activation barriers exerted on Na migration. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> calculated from GITT curves as a function of the state of charge during both sodiation and desodiation processes are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0008">8</a>. Both electrodes display a similar profile most of the time in the charging and discharging process. The calculated results demonstrate that the mean values of D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> are 1.02 × 10<sup>−10</sup>, 2.06 × 10<sup>−10</sup>, and 1.45 × 10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> for NFM, 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM and 1NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM, respectively. The much higher D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM demonstrates faster sodium-ion diffusion kinetics owing to the ion conductive coating.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/84972d33-cc41-4b27-96ae-8faed99692d4/smll202305019-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/84972d33-cc41-4b27-96ae-8faed99692d4/smll202305019-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/668c5bb7-d752-41d5-ac59-83108a5e64f2/smll202305019-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/84972d33-cc41-4b27-96ae-8faed99692d4/smll202305019-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">GITT curves and calculated diffusion efficiency of sodium-ion at different voltages for bare NFM and NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is tested to further investigate the effects of modification strategy on electrochemical resistances and kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305019-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305019-bib-0063_R_d49166958e1430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical EIS responses of the bare NFM and NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305019-fig-0009">9</a>, and the equivalent circuits fitted by Z View 2.0 software are inserted. The two semicircles are attributed to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and the electrified interface resistance (<i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>) between the electrode and electrolyte, respectively. The detailed data is listed in <b>Table</b>&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202305019-tbl-0001">1</a>. After 150 cycles, the increase of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> impedance of NaPO<sub>3</sub>-modified sample (519.1&nbsp;Ω) is much lower than the unmodified sample (1539&nbsp;Ω), indicating that the interfacial side reactions are significantly restrained due to the enhancement of crystal structure as well as the surface modification. The smaller impedance and higher D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> suggest that the structure of NaPO<sub>3</sub>-modified samples maintain well ordered after an extensive cycle. By combining the GITT and EIS results, it can be concluded that the introduction of NaPO<sub>3</sub> nanolayer does increase the electronic and ionic conductivity of the cathode material, thus promoting the electrochemical kinetics during charge/discharge processes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305019-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6dc66d7b-91e7-4470-b007-0b50a8452aaa/smll202305019-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6dc66d7b-91e7-4470-b007-0b50a8452aaa/smll202305019-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0bc5344d-2b53-4887-8d3e-cade925229ea/smll202305019-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6dc66d7b-91e7-4470-b007-0b50a8452aaa/smll202305019-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305019-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305019" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

               </figure>
            </section>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305019-sec-0050">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305019-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a facile solid-gas reaction utilizing gaseous P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> has been developed to form the ion conductive NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating layer with different thicknesses on the surface of the O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> particles without changing the bulk structure. Compared with the uncoated cathode particle, the NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated samples possess a stable interface between the electrode and the electrolyte solution as well as an unobstructed and short channel for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, especially in those samples with thinner thickness sodium metaphosphate coating. Electrochemical analyses demonstrated that NaPO<sub>3</sub> coating layer was effective in protecting active material from the corrosive electrolyte and thus improving the structure stability significantly, leading to a highly improved Na storage behavior in terms of average voltage, rate capability, and cycling stability. As a result, the 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM cathode exhibited its optimized cycling performance by an almost 20% capacity retention increase after 150 cycles in the wide voltage range of 2.0&nbsp;V–4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). This work supplies an insight into how to design a sodium metaphosphate coating layer for sodium-ion batteries cathode materials and presents a practical method for enhancing electrochemical performance of O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305019-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305019-sec-0060-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0070">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0080">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305019-sec-0090-title"> Material Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The hydroxide precursor Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> was donated by Zhongwei New Energy Material company (China) so that the same precursor could be utilized for all synthesis batches. The NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (abbreviated as NFM) samples were synthesized through the solid-state reaction between sodium carbonate and transition metal hydroxide Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>. The two precursors mixture was calcined at 500&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h, followed by 900&nbsp;°C annealing in an oxygen atmosphere for 10&nbsp;h to obtain the bare NFM products. To prepare the surface-modified NFM, 1&nbsp;g NFM, and 20&nbsp;mg P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (99.99% metal basis, Macklin Biochemical Co., Ltd.) were placed in two small alumina crucibles individually to prevent direct contact with each other and then were put in a couple of relatively large alumina crucibles that could slow down heated oxygen flow to guarantee the reaction performed completely. The crucibles were calcined at 360&nbsp;°C for 4&nbsp;h to sublimate P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> entirely and calcined at 500&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h to gain 0.5NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM samples. The heat treatment was performed under an oxygen flow rate of 0.5&nbsp;L&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> in a tube furnace to protect against the chemical valence reduction of transition metals and regulate the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> sublimation rate. In another set of experiments, the mass of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was doubled to obtain 1NaPO<sub>3</sub>-coated NFM samples. The bare NFM sample groups were applied with the same heat treatment protocol except for the use of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> source.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305019-sec-0100-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Surface morphologies and microstructure images were collected by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, S4800, Hitachi), energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS, XL-30, Philips), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM2100, JEOL). The structural characteristics of the as-prepared particles were determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD, D8 Advance, Bruker) with Cu Kα radiation source and scan range from 5° to 90°. Raman spectra were recorded on a Raman spectrometer (Renishaw In-Via Raman, Renishaw) with a laser wavelength of 488&nbsp;nm to characterize the surface composition of particles. The cycled cathode disks were removed from the coin cells, flushed three times with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) to dislodge electrolyte residues, laid in a vacuum oven for 8&nbsp;h, and then analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, AXIS SUPRA, Kratos). The binding energy scale was referenced to the main C 1s contribution of the amorphous carbon layer located at 284.8&nbsp;eV.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305019-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305019-sec-0110-title"> Electrochemical Measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The electrochemical characterizations were evaluated using CR2032-type coin half-cells assembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The cathode materials, conducting carbon black (Super P, SCM Chem. Shanghai, China), and binder poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF, Solvay) with a weight ratio of 80:10:10 were thoroughly mixed in N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, Aladdin, 99.9% purity) solvent for cathode slurries. The mixtures then were blade coated onto Al foils and vacuum-dried at 110&nbsp;°C for 4&nbsp;h to remove NMP completely. The dried cathode sheets were cut into circulars with a diameter of 14&nbsp;mm, and a mass loading density of active substance 5.33–5.85&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, which were then further dried at 110&nbsp;°C for 4&nbsp;h. For the Na metal anode, a sodium metal disk was diced from sodium cubes (Aldrich, 99% purity), which was flattened and punched out with a diameter of 14&nbsp;mm. The half-cells were assembled using an as-prepared cathode sheet, sodium foil as the anode, a Whatman glass microfiber filter, and 0.1&nbsp;mL electrolyte composed of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) and ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) containing 2% FEC (PC/EMC volume ratio = 1:1) (DoDo Chem, Shanghai, China). Charge–discharge experiments were tested and evaluated on a LAND cell test system (Wuhan, China) between 2.0 and 4.2&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 28&nbsp;°C. For cycling performance tests, the cells were cycled at 1&nbsp;C (1 C = 150&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) after buffer cycles to prevent overcharge from happening. For rate capability tests, different C-rates between 0.2 and 10&nbsp;C were applied on the cells for every 5 cycles after 2 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;C as buffer cycles. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) experiments were performed on an electrochemical workstation (Solartron-Analytical, 1470E) with the voltage amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV in the frequency range of between 100 and 0.01&nbsp;Hz. Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were carried out at 15&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> galvanostatic current pulse for 20&nbsp;min and a subsequent rest time of 3&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
